فللخارج. والغزل في معنى النتاج لأنه لا يتكرر وهو سبب لأولية الملك في المغزول والحنطة مما يتكرر فإن الإنسان قد يزرع في الأرض ثم يغر بل التراب فيميز الحنطة منها ثم تزرع ثانية، وكذا كل ما يكال أو يوزن والجبن لا يصنع إلا مرة وهو سبب لأولية الملك، وكذا اللبن إذا تنازعا في كونه حلب في ملكه والنخل يغرس غير مرة، فإذا تنازعا في أرض ونخيل في يد رجل فإنه يقضى للخارج بهما، وكذا في أرض مزروعه، أما إذا كان الزرع مما يتكرر فظاهر وإلا كان تبعاً للأرض ؛ كذا في الخلاصة وفيها لو أقام البينة على شاة في يد غيره أنها شاته وجز هذا الصوف منها وأقام ذو اليد أن الشاة التي يدعيها له منها فإنه يقضى بالشاة للمدعي لأنهما ادعيا في الشاة ملكاً مطلقاً فيقضى بالشاة للخارج ثم يتبعها الصوف لأن الجز ليس من أسباب الملك اهـ . والحاصل أن المنظور إليه من كونه يتكرر أولاً إنما هو الأصل لا التبع. وفي خزانة المفتين : شاتان في يد رجل إحداهما بيضاء والأخرى سوداء فادعاهما رجل وأقام البينة أنهما له وأن هذه البيضاء ولدت هذه السوداء في ملكه وأقام ذو اليد البينة أنهما له وأن هذه السوداء ولدت هذه البيضاء في ملكه فإنه يقضى لكل واحد منهما بالشاة التي ذكرت شهوده أنها ولدت في ملكه وإن كان في يد رجل حمام أو دجاج أو طير مما يفرخ أقام رجل البينة أنه له فرخ في ملكه وأقام صاحب اليد البينة على مثل ذلك قضى به لصاحب اليد ولو ادعى لبناً في يد رجل ضربه في ملكه وبرهن ذو اليد يقضى به للخارج، ولو كان مكان اللبن آجر أو جص أو نورة يقضى به لصاحب اليد وغزل القطن لا يتكرر فيقضى به لذي اليد بخلاف عزل الصوف وورق الشجر وثمرته مع النتاج بخلاف غصن الشجرة ،والحنطة ولا بد من الشهادة بالملك مع السبب الذي لا يتكرر كالنتاج، ولو برهن الخارج على أن البيضة التي تعلقت من هذه الدجاجة كانت له لم يقض له بالدجاجة ويقضى على صاحب الدجاجة ببيضة مثلها لصاحبها لأن ملك البيضة ليس بسبب لملك الدجاجة، فإن من غصب بيضة وحضنها تحت دجاجة له كان الفرخ للغاصب وعليه مثلها بخلاف الأمة فإن ولدها لصاحب الأم وجلد الشاة يقضى به لصاحب اليد، والجبة المحشوة والفرو وكل ما يقطع من الثياب والبسط والأنماط والثوب المصبوغ بعصفر أو زعفران يقضى بها للخارج .اهـ. الثالثة برهن الخارج على الملك المطلق وذو اليد على الشراء منه فذو اليد أولى لأن الأول وإن كان يدعي أولية الملك فهذا تلقي منه، وفي هذه لا تنافي كما إذا أقر بالملك له ثم ادعى الشراء منه. وأشار المؤلف رحمه الله إلى أن الخارج لو برهن أن فلاناً القاضي قضى له بهذه الأمة بشهود أنها له وبرهن ذو اليد على النتاج فإنه يقدم الخارج وهو قولهما لأن القضاء صح ظاهراً فلا ينقض ما لم يظهر خطؤه بيقين ولم يظهر لاحتمال أنه اشتراها من ذي اليد. وعند محمد يقضى بها لذي اليد كذا في الكافي. وهذا إذا لم يبينوا القضاء فإن بينوه فإن شهدوا أن القاضي أقر عندهم أنه قضى بشهادة شهود أنها له أو بالنتاج فإنه ينقض القضاء اتفاقاً، وإن شهدوا أنه قضى له بالنتاج ببينة ولم يشهدوا على إقرار القاضي لا ينقض القضاء لاحتمال القضاء بالشراء من ذي اليد ؛ كذا في خزانة المفتين . قوله: (ولو برهن كل على الشراء من الآخر ولا تاريخ سقطا وتترك الدار في يد ذي اليد) وهذا عندهما، وعلى قول محمد يقضى بالبينتين ويكون للخارج لأن العمل بهما ممكن فيجعل كأنه اشترى ذو اليد من الآخر وقبض ثم باع لأن القبض دلالة السبق، ولا يعكس الأمر لأن البيع قبل القبض لا يجوز وإن كان في العقار عنده ولهما أن الإقدام على الشراء إقرار منه بالملك للبائع فصار كأنهما قامتا على الإقرارين وفيه التهاتر بالإجماع كذا هنا، ولأن السبب يراد لحكمه وهو الملك ولا يمكن القضاء لذي اليد إلا بملك مستحق فبقي القضاء له بمجرد السبب وأنه لا يفيده ثم لو شهدت البينتان على نقد الثمن فالألف بالألف قصاص هذه تصلح أماً لهذه كانت علامة الصدق ظاهرة في شهادة شهود أحدهما فيقضى بشهادة شهوده. وعن أبي يوسف فيما إذا كان سن الشاتين مشكلاً إني لا أقبل بينتهما وأقضي بالشاة لكل واحد منهما بالشاة التي في يده وهذا قضاء ترك لا قضاء استحقاق. ولو أقام الذي في يده البيضاء أن البيضاء شاتي ولدت في ملكي والسوداء التي في يد صاحبي شاتي ولدت من هذه البيضاء، وأقام الذي السوداء في يده أن السوداء ولدت في ملكي والبيضاء التي في يد صاحبي ملكي ولدت من السوداء فإنه يقضى لكل واحد منهما بما في يده اهـ. ولو كانت في أيديهما فهي للثاني. ولو برهنا على نتاج دابة وأرخا قضى لمن وافق سنها تاريخه، وإن أشكل ذلك فلهما ولو برهن أحد الخارجين على الغصب والآخر على الوديعة استويا والراكب واللابس أحق من آخذ اللجام والكم، وصاحب الحمل والجذوع والاتصال أحق من الغير ثوب في يده وطرفه في يد آخر نصف. صبي يعبر عن
Book of Claims: Chapter of Claim of Two Men. And cutting of wool: He answered in al-Kafi that it is like the description of the sheep and it was not wealth except after cutting, so its sale before cutting is not permissible. And the sword blade is asked about; if they inform that it strikes only once, it belongs to the possessor; otherwise, to the outside claimant. Yarn is in the meaning of offspring because it does not recur, and it is a cause for the first ownership in the spun product. Wheat is of the recurring kind, for a person may sow in the land then [harvest] and separate the wheat from the soil and then sow again. Likewise everything that is measured by volume or weight. Cheese is made only once and is a cause for first ownership; likewise milk if they dispute whether it was milked in his ownership. Palm trees are planted more than once; so if they dispute about land and palm trees in the hand of a man, judgment is given to the outside claimant for both; likewise for land with crops. As for crops that recur, it is apparent; otherwise it is subsidiary to the land. Thus in al-Khulasa. And in it: If he presents evidence about a sheep in the hand of another that it is his sheep and that this wool was cut from it, and the possessor presents evidence that the sheep he claims from him has [wool] from it, then judgment is given for the sheep to the plaintiff because both claimed absolute ownership of the sheep, so the sheep is judged for the outside claimant; then the wool follows because cutting is not among the causes of ownership. End. The upshot is that what is considered regarding recurrence or not is the origin, not the subsidiary. In Khizanat al-Muftiyin: Two sheep in the hand of a man, one white and one black. A man claims both and presents evidence that they are his and that this white one gave birth to this black one in his ownership; and the possessor presents evidence that they are his and that this black one gave birth to this white one in his ownership. Then judgment is given to each of them for the sheep that his witnesses mentioned was born in his ownership. And if pigeons, chickens, or birds that lay eggs are in the hand of a man, and a man presents evidence that a chick in his ownership is his, and the possessor presents evidence to the same effect, judgment is given for the possessor. And if he claims milk in the hand of a man that he milked it in his ownership, and the possessor proves [otherwise], judgment is given for the outside claimant. If instead of milk there is brick, gypsum, or lime, judgment is given for the possessor. Yarn of cotton does not recur, so judgment is given for the possessor, unlike the combing of wool, leaves of a tree and its fruit along with the offspring, unlike the branch of a tree. And wheat: evidence of ownership with the cause that does not recur, like offspring, is necessary. And if the outside claimant proves that the egg which hatched from this chicken was his, judgment is not given for the chicken, but the owner of the chicken is ordered to give a like egg to the owner because ownership of the egg is not a cause for ownership of the chicken. For if someone usurps an egg and hatches it under his chicken, the chick belongs to the usurper and he must pay a like egg, unlike the slave woman, for her child belongs to the owner of the mother. And the sheep's skin is judged for the possessor. The stuffed cloak, fur, and everything cut from garments, carpets, and spreads, and cloth dyed with safflower or saffron are judged for the outside claimant. End. Third: The outside claimant proves absolute ownership and the possessor proves purchase from him; then the possessor is preferred because the first, though claiming first ownership, this one acquired from him; and in this there is no contradiction, as if he acknowledged ownership to him then claimed purchase from him. And the author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated that if the outside claimant proves that so-and-so the judge ruled for him regarding this slave woman based on witnesses that she is his, and the possessor proves offspring, then the outside claimant is given precedence, and this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) because the judgment is valid outwardly and is not overturned unless its error is certain, and it is not certain due to the possibility that he bought her from the possessor. According to Muhammad, judgment is given for the possessor, as in al-Kafi. This is if they did not specify the judgment; if they specify it, then if they witness that the judge acknowledged to them that he ruled based on witnesses that she is his or on offspring, the judgment is overturned by agreement. If they witness that he ruled for him on offspring with evidence and did not witness the judge's acknowledgment, the judgment is not overturned due to the possibility that the judgment was based on purchase from the possessor. Thus in Khizanat al-Muftiyin. His statement: (And if each proves purchase from the other with no date, both are rejected and the house is left in the hand of the possessor). This is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?), while according to Muhammad, judgment is given with both evidences and the house goes to the outside claimant because acting on both is possible, so it is as if the possessor bought from the other and took possession then sold, because possession indicates priority; and the reverse does not hold because sale before possession is not permissible, even in real estate according to him. And the two say that the act of buying is an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, so it is as if both evidences are based on acknowledgments, and there is mutual nullification by consensus. Also, the cause is intended for its effect, which is ownership, and it is not possible to judge for the possessor except through earned ownership, so judgment for him remains based on mere cause, which does not benefit him. Then if both evidences testify to the payment of the price, then the thousand is offset by the thousand. This [sheep] can be a mother for that one; the sign of truth is apparent in the evidence of one's witnesses, so judgment is given based on his witnesses. And from Abu Yusuf: regarding when the age of the two sheep is ambiguous, I do not accept both evidences and judge each sheep for the one in whose hand it is; this is a judgment of abandonment, not a judgment of entitlement. And if the one in whose hand is the white sheep presents evidence that the white sheep is his sheep born in his ownership and the black sheep in the hand of my companion is my sheep born from this white one, and the one in whose hand is the black sheep presents evidence that the black sheep is born in his ownership and the white sheep in the hand of my companion is my property born from the black one, then judgment is given to each for what is in his hand. End. And if they were in both hands, it belongs to the second. And if they both prove offspring of an animal and date it, judgment is given to the one whose age matches the date; if that is ambiguous, then it is for both. And if one outside claimant proves usurpation and the other proves deposit, they are equal. And the rider and wearer have more right than the one who takes the bridle and sleeve. And the owner of the load, beams, and attachment has more right than others. A garment in his hand and its edge in another's hand: half. A boy expresses
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses the intricate rules of evidence when two parties claim ownership of the same object, particularly when the object is produced from another (like wool, milk, offspring). The core principle is that when both parties present evidence, the judgment depends on whether the cause of ownership is recurring or non-recurring. A non-recurring cause (like giving birth, making cheese, spinning yarn) establishes original ownership in the one who caused it, so if each claims a non-recurring cause, the possessor's evidence is preferred because it aligns with the apparent state. If the cause is recurring (like farming wheat), the outside claimant's evidence is taken because the land is the origin. The passage then discusses specific cases: two sheep where each claims the other was born in his ownership; here each gets the sheep his witnesses mentioned as born in his ownership, unless the ages are ambiguous, in which case each gets what he holds (Abu Yusuf's view). For eggs, ownership of the egg does not transfer to the chicken; if evidence is given, the possessor keeps the chicken but must compensate for the egg. For clothes and dyed items, the outside claimant prevails. The latter part of the passage deals with competing claims of purchase: if both claim to have bought from the other without date, the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say both are rejected and the current possessor keeps it; Muhammad says give it to the outside claimant because the claims can be reconciled by considering the order of possession and sale. Also discussed is the effect of a court judgment: if a prior judgment exists, it is not overturned without certainty of error. Throughout, the passage provides detailed rules for judges in evidentiary conflicts, reflecting the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to balancing competing proof.

Key Terms
نتاج (nitaaj): Offspring of animals, considered a non-recurring cause of ownership
تهاتر (tahaatur): Mutual cancellation of claims or debts
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The current possessor
خارج (khaarij): The outside claimant

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position, when both parties prove purchase from each other without date, what is the outcome?
A) The property is divided equally.
B) The property is given to the outside claimant.
C) Both claims are rejected and the property stays with the possessor.
D) The judge orders a new sale.

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi view on mutual purchase without date is that both claims are rejected and the property remains with the possessor.

Shahadat — Testimony
advanced
15 min
Conflicting Offspring Claims and the Effect of Prior Court Judgment
Rules for conflicting evidence of offspring and interaction with court judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule when two parties present evidence of offspring (nitaaj) and analyze the interaction with a prior court judgment, including the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
When both parties prove offspring, each gets the animal linked to his birth; a prior judgment stands unless error is certain.

Disputes over offspring (nitaaj) are common in animal ownership. When both sides have evidence, the Hanafi school gives each the animal his witnesses linked to his own birth. Additionally, if a court has already ruled, that judgment is not easily overturned. This lesson covers these rules and a related dispute with Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of offspring claim
Is it about the same animal being born from two mothers? Or about eggs?
2
Apply the rule for conflicting offspring
Each party gets the animal his witnesses said was born in his ownership.
This applies to sheep, pigeons, etc.
3
Handle ambiguous ages (Abu Yusuf)
If the ages of the animals are unclear, each keeps what he possesses, as a judgment of abandonment.
Abu Yusuf's lone view.
4
Consider prior court judgment
If an outside claimant has a judgment based on witnesses, and the possessor later proves offspring, the judgment stands (majority) unless error is certain.
Muhammad disagrees and would overturn the judgment.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
For the prior judgment scenario, the majority view is mufta bihi.

يقدم الخارج وهو قولهما لأن القضاء صح ظاهراً فلا ينقض ما لم يظهر خطؤه بيقين ولم يظهر لاحتمال أنه اشتراها من ذي اليد. وعند محمد يقضى بها لذي اليد كذا في الكافي. وهذا إذا لم يبينوا القضاء فإن بينوه فإن شهدوا أن القاضي أقر عندهم أنه قضى بشهادة شهود أنها له أو بالنتاج فإنه ينقض القضاء اتفاقاً، وإن شهدوا أنه قضى له بالنتاج ببينة ولم يشهدوا على إقرار القاضي لا ينقض القضاء لاحتمال القضاء بالشراء من ذي اليد ؛ كذا في خزانة المفتين . قوله: (ولو برهن كل على الشراء من الآخر ولا تاريخ سقطا وتترك الدار في يد ذي اليد) وهذا عندهما، وعلى قول محمد يقضى بالبينتين ويكون للخارج لأن العمل بهما ممكن فيجعل كأنه اشترى ذو اليد من الآخر وقبض ثم باع لأن القبض دلالة السبق، ولا يعكس الأمر لأن البيع قبل القبض لا يجوز وإن كان في العقار عنده ولهما أن الإقدام على الشراء إقرار منه بالملك للبائع فصار كأنهما قامتا على الإقرارين وفيه التهاتر بالإجماع كذا هنا، ولأن السبب يراد لحكمه وهو الملك ولا يمكن القضاء لذي اليد إلا بملك مستحق فبقي القضاء له بمجرد السبب وأنه لا يفيده ثم لو شهدت البينتان على نقد الثمن فالألف بالألف قصاص هذه تصلح أماً لهذه كانت علامة الصدق ظاهرة في شهادة شهود أحدهما فيقضى بشهادة شهوده. وعن أبي يوسف فيما إذا كان سن الشاتين مشكلاً إني لا أقبل بينتهما وأقضي بالشاة لكل واحد منهما بالشاة التي في يده وهذا قضاء ترك لا قضاء استحقاق. ولو أقام الذي في يده البيضاء أن البيضاء شاتي ولدت في ملكي والسوداء التي في يد صاحبي شاتي ولدت من هذه البيضاء، وأقام الذي السوداء في يده أن السوداء ولدت في ملكي والبيضاء التي في يد صاحبي ملكي ولدت من السوداء فإنه يقضى لكل واحد منهما بما في يده اهـ. ولو كانت في أيديهما فهي للثاني. ولو برهنا على نتاج دابة وأرخا قضى لمن وافق سنها تاريخه، وإن أشكل ذلك فلهما ولو برهن أحد الخارجين على الغصب والآخر على الوديعة استويا والراكب واللابس أحق من آخذ اللجام والكم، وصاحب الحمل والجذوع والاتصال أحق من الغير ثوب في يده وطرفه في يد آخر نصف. صبي يعبر عن
Book of Claims: Chapter of Claim of Two Men. And cutting of wool: He answered in al-Kafi that it is like the description of the sheep and it was not wealth except after cutting, so its sale before cutting is not permissible. And the sword blade is asked about; if they inform that it strikes only once, it belongs to the possessor; otherwise, to the outside claimant. Yarn is in the meaning of offspring because it does not recur, and it is a cause for the first ownership in the spun product. Wheat is of the recurring kind, for a person may sow in the land then [harvest] and separate the wheat from the soil and then sow again. Likewise everything that is measured by volume or weight. Cheese is made only once and is a cause for first ownership; likewise milk if they dispute whether it was milked in his ownership. Palm trees are planted more than once; so if they dispute about land and palm trees in the hand of a man, judgment is given to the outside claimant for both; likewise for land with crops. As for crops that recur, it is apparent; otherwise it is subsidiary to the land. Thus in al-Khulasa. And in it: If he presents evidence about a sheep in the hand of another that it is his sheep and that this wool was cut from it, and the possessor presents evidence that the sheep he claims from him has [wool] from it, then judgment is given for the sheep to the plaintiff because both claimed absolute ownership of the sheep, so the sheep is judged for the outside claimant; then the wool follows because cutting is not among the causes of ownership. End. The upshot is that what is considered regarding recurrence or not is the origin, not the subsidiary. In Khizanat al-Muftiyin: Two sheep in the hand of a man, one white and one black. A man claims both and presents evidence that they are his and that this white one gave birth to this black one in his ownership; and the possessor presents evidence that they are his and that this black one gave birth to this white one in his ownership. Then judgment is given to each of them for the sheep that his witnesses mentioned was born in his ownership. And if pigeons, chickens, or birds that lay eggs are in the hand of a man, and a man presents evidence that a chick in his ownership is his, and the possessor presents evidence to the same effect, judgment is given for the possessor. And if he claims milk in the hand of a man that he milked it in his ownership, and the possessor proves [otherwise], judgment is given for the outside claimant. If instead of milk there is brick, gypsum, or lime, judgment is given for the possessor. Yarn of cotton does not recur, so judgment is given for the possessor, unlike the combing of wool, leaves of a tree and its fruit along with the offspring, unlike the branch of a tree. And wheat: evidence of ownership with the cause that does not recur, like offspring, is necessary. And if the outside claimant proves that the egg which hatched from this chicken was his, judgment is not given for the chicken, but the owner of the chicken is ordered to give a like egg to the owner because ownership of the egg is not a cause for ownership of the chicken. For if someone usurps an egg and hatches it under his chicken, the chick belongs to the usurper and he must pay a like egg, unlike the slave woman, for her child belongs to the owner of the mother. And the sheep's skin is judged for the possessor. The stuffed cloak, fur, and everything cut from garments, carpets, and spreads, and cloth dyed with safflower or saffron are judged for the outside claimant. End. Third: The outside claimant proves absolute ownership and the possessor proves purchase from him; then the possessor is preferred because the first, though claiming first ownership, this one acquired from him; and in this there is no contradiction, as if he acknowledged ownership to him then claimed purchase from him. And the author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated that if the outside claimant proves that so-and-so the judge ruled for him regarding this slave woman based on witnesses that she is his, and the possessor proves offspring, then the outside claimant is given precedence, and this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) because the judgment is valid outwardly and is not overturned unless its error is certain, and it is not certain due to the possibility that he bought her from the possessor. According to Muhammad, judgment is given for the possessor, as in al-Kafi. This is if they did not specify the judgment; if they specify it, then if they witness that the judge acknowledged to them that he ruled based on witnesses that she is his or on offspring, the judgment is overturned by agreement. If they witness that he ruled for him on offspring with evidence and did not witness the judge's acknowledgment, the judgment is not overturned due to the possibility that the judgment was based on purchase from the possessor. Thus in Khizanat al-Muftiyin. His statement: (And if each proves purchase from the other with no date, both are rejected and the house is left in the hand of the possessor). This is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?), while according to Muhammad, judgment is given with both evidences and the house goes to the outside claimant because acting on both is possible, so it is as if the possessor bought from the other and took possession then sold, because possession indicates priority; and the reverse does not hold because sale before possession is not permissible, even in real estate according to him. And the two say that the act of buying is an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, so it is as if both evidences are based on acknowledgments, and there is mutual nullification by consensus. Also, the cause is intended for its effect, which is ownership, and it is not possible to judge for the possessor except through earned ownership, so judgment for him remains based on mere cause, which does not benefit him. Then if both evidences testify to the payment of the price, then the thousand is offset by the thousand. This [sheep] can be a mother for that one; the sign of truth is apparent in the evidence of one's witnesses, so judgment is given based on his witnesses. And from Abu Yusuf: regarding when the age of the two sheep is ambiguous, I do not accept both evidences and judge each sheep for the one in whose hand it is; this is a judgment of abandonment, not a judgment of entitlement. And if the one in whose hand is the white sheep presents evidence that the white sheep is his sheep born in his ownership and the black sheep in the hand of my companion is my sheep born from this white one, and the one in whose hand is the black sheep presents evidence that the black sheep is born in his ownership and the white sheep in the hand of my companion is my property born from the black one, then judgment is given to each for what is in his hand. End. And if they were in both hands, it belongs to the second. And if they both prove offspring of an animal and date it, judgment is given to the one whose age matches the date; if that is ambiguous, then it is for both. And if one outside claimant proves usurpation and the other proves deposit, they are equal. And the rider and wearer have more right than the one who takes the bridle and sleeve. And the owner of the load, beams, and attachment has more right than others. A garment in his hand and its edge in another's hand: half. A boy expresses
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses the intricate rules of evidence when two parties claim ownership of the same object, particularly when the object is produced from another (like wool, milk, offspring). The core principle is that when both parties present evidence, the judgment depends on whether the cause of ownership is recurring or non-recurring. A non-recurring cause (like giving birth, making cheese, spinning yarn) establishes original ownership in the one who caused it, so if each claims a non-recurring cause, the possessor's evidence is preferred because it aligns with the apparent state. If the cause is recurring (like farming wheat), the outside claimant's evidence is taken because the land is the origin. The passage then discusses specific cases: two sheep where each claims the other was born in his ownership; here each gets the sheep his witnesses mentioned as born in his ownership, unless the ages are ambiguous, in which case each gets what he holds (Abu Yusuf's view). For eggs, ownership of the egg does not transfer to the chicken; if evidence is given, the possessor keeps the chicken but must compensate for the egg. For clothes and dyed items, the outside claimant prevails. The latter part of the passage deals with competing claims of purchase: if both claim to have bought from the other without date, the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say both are rejected and the current possessor keeps it; Muhammad says give it to the outside claimant because the claims can be reconciled by considering the order of possession and sale. Also discussed is the effect of a court judgment: if a prior judgment exists, it is not overturned without certainty of error. Throughout, the passage provides detailed rules for judges in evidentiary conflicts, reflecting the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to balancing competing proof.

Key Terms
نتاج (nitaaj): Offspring of animals
قضاء (qada'): Court judgment
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): Possessor
خارج (khaarij): Outside claimant

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the effect of the possessor proving offspring after an outside claimant has already obtained a court judgment based on witnesses?
A) The judgment is automatically overturned.
B) The judgment stands because its error is not certain.
C) The case is sent back for retrial.
D) The judgment is enforced but the possessor gets compensation.

Key Takeaway
In offspring disputes, each claimant gets the animal linked to his birth; a prior judgment is not overturned by later offspring evidence unless the judgment's error is certain.
